No. 150? 150 is 2*3*5^2, not divisible by 4, but f(150)=? 150: proper divisors: 1,2,3,5,6,10,15,25,30,50,75. Three largest: 75,50,30 sum=155, then 155->? 155=5*31, sum=1+5+31=37, prime, fails. So 150 fails. So the safe numbers seem to be those that are multiples of 6 and also have the property that they are either fixed points (i.e., not divisible by 4 or 5) or they are multiples of 72? Wait, 72 is multiple of 6 and also divisible by 4. 216 is also multiple of 6 and divisible by 8? Actually 216=2^3*3^3, divisible by 8, so it's in the family 72t with t odd. But also 72 itself works. So it seems that if N is a multiple of 6 and the exponent of 2 in N is at least 2, then it might work only if N/12 is a multiple of 6? That is, N is multiple of 72. But also what about N=2^3*3*? 72=2^3*3^2, so N=2^3*3^2*m with m odd and not divisible by 5? But we also have 216=2^3*3^3, which is also multiple of 72. So the condition N multiple of 72 seems necessary for safety in the s3=4 case. But is it also sufficient? We need to check if all N that are multiples of 72 and also not divisible by 5? Actually if N=72, it's fine; if N=72*3=216, fine; if N=72*5=360, but 360 is divisible by 5? 360=2^3*3^2*5, so it has factor 5. Then s3? For N=360, divisors: 2,3,4,5,6,8,9,10,12,15,18,20,24,30,36,40,45,60,72,90,120,180,360. s1=2,s2=3,s3=4. So f=13*(360/12)=13*30=390. 390=2*3*5*13, proper divisors: 1,2,3,5,6,10,13,15,26,30,39,65,78,130,195,390? Actually largest proper: 195,130,78 sum=403? Wait compute f(390): divisors: 2,3,5,6,10,13,15,26,30,39,65,78,130,195,390. Three largest: 195,130,78 sum=403. 403=13*31? Actually 13*31=403, so 403 prime? 403=13*31, so it's composite. Then f(403)=? 403=13*31, proper: 1,13,31, sum=45? Actually largest proper: 31,13,1 sum=45. 45=3^2*5, proper: 1,3,5,9,15,45? Actually 45: proper divisors 1,3,5,9,15. Three largest: 15,9,5 sum=29, prime. So fails. So 360 fails. So for N=72t, we also need that t is not divisible by 5? Because if t is divisible by 5, then N is divisible by 5, and we saw that leads to failure. So t must be coprime to 5. Also, t odd? Since 72 has factor 2^3, N=72t, if t even, then exponent of 2 increases, but s3 still 4? Actually if t even, say t=2, N=144, we already saw 144 fails. So t even likely fails. So t must be odd. Also, t not divisible by 5. So N=72*t with t odd and not divisible by 5. That gives infinite family.

Now, what about numbers that are even but not divisible by 3? For example, N=4? 4 has proper divisors: 1,2 only, so fails. N=8 fails. N=10 fails. N=14 fails. N=16 fails. N=20 fails. N=22 fails. N=26 fails. N=28 fails. N=32 fails. N=34? 34=2*17, proper: 1,2,17 sum=20, then 20->19 prime fails. So probably no safe numbers in this category. But we need to check N=2^a * p^b with p odd? Might be that they all eventually lead to a prime or square of prime. However, we should test N=2^2*5=20 fails; N=2^2*7=28 fails; N=2^2*11=44 fails; N=2^2*13=52? 52=2^2*13, proper divisors: 1,2,4,13,26. Three largest: 26,13,4 sum=43 prime fails. N=2^2*17=68? 68=2^2*17, divisors: 1,2,4,17,34,68. Three largest: 34,17,4 sum=55, 55=5*11, then f(55)=1+5+11=17 prime fails. So no.

What about N=2^3*5=40? 40 fails. N=2^3*7=56? fails. N=2^3*11=88? 88=8*11, proper: 1,2,4,8,11,22,44. Three largest: 44,22,11 sum=77, 77=7*11, f(77)=1+7+11=19 prime fails. So all even numbers not divisible by 3 seem to fail.

Thus the only possible a1 are those that are even and divisible by 3. So we restrict to multiples of 6.

Now, among multiples of 6, we have two types: those with exponent of 2 = 1 (i.e., not divisible by 4) and those with exponent of 2 >= 2. For exponent of 2 = 1, the number is of the form 2*3*m, where m is odd and not divisible by 5? Actually for s3=6, we need that there is no divisor between 3 and 6, i.e., not divisible by 4 or 5. So m odd and not divisible by 5. Those are fixed points, safe. For exponent of 2 >= 2, the number is divisible by 4. Then s3=4 (since 2,3,4 are divisors). So f(N) = (13/12)N. Write N = 12k, then f=13k. So the orbit goes to a number of the form 13k. For N to be safe, we need that 13k is safe. So we need to analyze numbers of the form 13k.

We need to solve: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n >= 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a1."

Interpretation: We have an infinite sequence of positive integers, each term has at least three proper divisors. For each n, a_{n+1} is defined as the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. So we need to find all a1 such that this sequence can be defined indefinitely (i.e., for each n, a_n has at least three proper divisors, so the sum is defined, and then the next term also has at least three proper divisors, etc.). It's an infinite sequence, so we need to ensure that the process never terminates (i.e., we always have at least three proper divisors). However, it's possible that a_n becomes a prime? But the condition says each a_n has at least three proper divisors. So a_n cannot be prime (which has only one proper divisor, 1) nor a prime power? Actually a prime power p^k has proper divisors: 1, p, p^2, ..., p^{k-1} if k>=2. So for k=2, p^2 has proper divisors 1 and p (only two). So it needs at least three proper divisors, so the number must have at least three proper divisors. That means it must have at least four divisors total? Actually the proper divisors are divisors except the number itself. So if N has d(N) divisors, then proper divisors count = d(N)-1. So we need d(N)-1 >= 3 => d(N) >= 4. So numbers with at least 4 divisors. That includes composite numbers that are not prime powers of exponent 2? Actually any number with at least 3 proper divisors: e.g., 6 has proper divisors 1,2,3 (3 proper divisors). So 6 works. 8 has proper divisors 1,2,4 (3 proper divisors). 12 has 1,2,3,4,6 (5 proper divisors). So the condition excludes primes and squares of primes (p^2) because they have exactly 2 proper divisors (1 and p). Also numbers like 1 have no proper divisors. So each a_n must be composite and not a square of a prime? Actually p^2 has exactly 2 proper divisors: 1 and p. So need at least 3 proper divisors => d(N) >= 4. So N can be p^3 (divisors 1,p,p^2: that's 3 proper divisors? Actually p^3: divisors 1, p, p^2, p^3. Proper: 1,p,p^2 => 3 proper divisors. So p^3 works. So condition: N must have at least 4 divisors, i.e., not prime and not square of prime. Actually p^2 has 3 divisors total, so proper count =2. So exclude p^2. p^3 has 4 divisors total, proper=3. So included. So a_n can be any number with at least 4 divisors.

Now the recurrence: a_{n+1} = sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n. We need the sequence to be infinite, so each term must have at least three proper divisors. We need to find all possible a1 that allow this.

We can explore properties. Let's denote for a positive integer N, let d1, d2, d3, ... be all proper divisors in increasing order? The three largest proper divisors: we need to consider divisors of N, excluding N itself. The three largest proper divisors are N/p1, N/p2, N/p3? Actually if N has divisors, the largest proper divisor is N/ smallest prime factor? Typically the largest proper divisor is N/ smallest prime divisor (since if p is smallest prime divisor, then N/p is a divisor, and it's the largest proper divisor). But careful: If N is not prime, let p be smallest prime factor. Actually need to check: The divisors come in pairs d and N/d. The largest proper divisor is the largest divisor less than N. It is N / smallest prime divisor. Because the smallest divisor greater than 1 is p, and its complement N/p is the largest divisor less than N. So that's correct.

Now the second largest proper divisor: It could be N / next smallest prime divisor? Or maybe N / some divisor? But careful: The divisors in descending order: N/p, then maybe N/q where q is next smallest divisor? But if N has a divisor d that is not prime, then N/d might not be integer? Actually if d is a divisor, N/d is also a divisor. So the largest proper divisor is N/p. No. For N with at least two distinct prime factors, the smallest prime factor p gives the largest proper divisor N/p. The second smallest prime factor q gives N/q. But N/q might be smaller than N/p? Since q >= p, N/q <= N/p. So the descending order of divisors is: N/p, N/q, ... but there could be other divisors like N/p^2? That's smaller. So the three largest proper divisors are likely N/p, N/q, N/r? But if N is a prime power p^k, then proper divisors are p^{k-1}, p^{k-2}, ..., p, 1. The largest proper is p^{k-1}. The second largest is p^{k-2}. The third largest is p^{k-3}. So they are powers of p. But careful: If N has prime factors p < q < r, then N/p, N/q, N/r are divisors. But N/p is largest, N/q next, N/r next. Could there be a divisor like N/(pq)? That's smaller than N/q? Since N/(pq) = (N/p)/q < N/p. And N/(pq) might be greater than N/r? Possibly if r is much larger? But we need the three largest. Usually they are N/p, N/q, N/r if N has at least three distinct prime factors. But if N has exactly two distinct prime factors, say p<q, then the divisors are of the form p^i q^j. Actually need to compare: N/p = p^{k-1} q^j, N/q = p^k q^{j-1}. Which is larger? Since p<q, compare exponents: p^{k-1} q^j vs p^k q^{j-1}. Divide by p^{k-1} q^{j-1}: we get q vs p. So N/p > N/q if q>p, which is true. So N/p > N/q. Next, after N/q, the next largest could be N/(pq)? That is p^{k-1} q^{j-1} if k>=1, j>=1. But compare N/(pq) and N/q? We need to determine all possible a1 such that the sequence is infinite (i.e., each term has at least three proper divisors). Possibly the sequence becomes periodic or eventually constant? Might be that only certain numbers can produce infinite sequences.

Let's try to characterize the recurrence.

Let f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N.

We need f(N) to be defined (i.e., N has at least 3 proper divisors) and then also f(N) must have at least 3 proper divisors, and so on.

We need to find all initial N such that iterating f yields infinite sequence of numbers each with at least 4 divisors? Actually each term must have at least three proper divisors, so the condition holds for all terms. It might be that for some N, after some steps we get a number with fewer than three proper divisors, then the sequence cannot be defined further (the problem says "the infinite sequence ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n, a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n." So the sequence is given to be infinite, so we must have that for every n, a_n has at least three proper divisors, and then a_{n+1} is defined accordingly. So we are to find all a1 for which there exists an infinite sequence satisfying these conditions. It might be that for some a1, the sequence eventually hits a number that has fewer than three proper divisors, but then the sequence would not be infinite; so those a1 are not allowed.

So we need to characterize all a1 such that the orbit under f stays within the set of numbers with at least 3 proper divisors (i.e., at least 4 divisors). Possibly the only possible a1 are numbers that are perfect numbers? Or something like that.

<solution>
We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- We recognized that the sequence is deterministic and each term must have at least three proper divisors.
- We analyzed small examples and observed that numbers of the form \(2\cdot3\cdot m\) with \(m\) odd and not divisible by \(5\) are fixed points.
- We attempted to characterize the set of possible \(a_1\) but found that the dynamics are complex.

We are not able to complete the rigorous solution. Therefore, we cannot provide a correct proof.
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is incomplete and contains gaps. We did not provide a complete rigorous argument; we only described partial observations.
